He also orchestrated education programs and implemented measures to increase female political participation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> Chiang viewed the Communists as a greater threat than the Japanese and returned to Jiangxi, where he initiated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fifth_Encirclement_Campaign_against_Jiangxi_Soviet" title="Fifth Encirclement Campaign against Jiangxi Soviet">fifth encirclement campaign</a>, which involved the construction of a concrete and barbed wire "wall of fire" around the state, which was accompanied by aerial bombardment, to which Zhou's tactics proved ineffective. Trapped inside, morale among the Red Army dropped as food and medicine became scarce. The leadership decided to evacuate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Long_March:_1934.E2.80.931935"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Long_March:_1934–1935">Long March: 1934–1935</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Long_March_1934-1935-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="857" data-file-width="1119" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Map_of_the_Long_March_1934-1935-en.svg/250px-Map_of_the_Long_March_1934-1935-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Map_of_the_Long_March_1934-1935-en.svg/375px-Map_of_the_Long_March_1934-1935-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Map_of_the_Long_March_1934-1935-en.svg/500px-Map_of_the_Long_March_1934-1935-en.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Long_March_1934-1935-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An overview map of the Long March</div></div></div>
<p>On 14 October 1934, the Red Army broke through the KMT line on the Jiangxi Soviet's south-west corner at Xinfeng with 85,000 soldiers and 15,000 party cadres and embarked on the "<a href="/wiki/Long_March" title="Long March">Long March</a>". In order to make the escape, many of the wounded and the ill, as well as women and children, were left behind, defended by a group of guerrilla fighters whom the KMT massacred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feigon_2002_57_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feigon_2002_57-134">[133]</a></sup> The 100,000 who escaped headed to southern Hunan, first crossing the <a href="/wiki/Xiang_River" title="Xiang River">Xiang River</a> after heavy fighting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feigon_2002_57_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Feigon_2002_57-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> and then the <a href="/wiki/Wu_River_(Yuan_River,_north)" title="Wu River (Yuan River, north)">Wu River</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Guizhou" title="Guizhou">Guizhou</a> where they took <a href="/wiki/Zunyi" title="Zunyi">Zunyi</a> in January 1935. Temporarily resting in the city, they <a href="/wiki/Zunyi_Conference" title="Zunyi Conference">held a conference</a>; here, Mao was elected to a position of leadership, becoming Chairman of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Politburo_of_the_Communist_Party_of_China" title="Politburo of the Communist Party of China">the Politburo</a>, and <i>de facto</i> leader of both Party and Red Army, in part because his candidacy was supported by Soviet Premier <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>. Insisting that they operate as a guerrilla force, he laid out a destination: the Shenshi Soviet in <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, Northern China, from where the Communists could focus on fighting the Japanese. Mao believed that in focusing on the anti-imperialist struggle, the Communists would earn the trust of the Chinese people, who in turn would renounce the KMT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>From Zunyi, Mao led his troops to <a href="/wiki/Loushan_Pass" title="Loushan Pass">Loushan Pass</a>, where they faced armed opposition but successfully crossed the river. Chiang flew into the area to lead his armies against Mao, but the Communists outmanoeuvred him and crossed the <a href="/wiki/Jinsha_River" title="Jinsha River">Jinsha River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> Faced with the more difficult task of crossing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tatu_River" title="Tatu River">Tatu River</a>, they managed it by fighting a battle over the <a href="/wiki/Luding_Bridge" title="Luding Bridge">Luding Bridge</a> in May, taking <a href="/wiki/Luding_County" title="Luding County">Luding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> Marching through the mountain ranges around <a href="/wiki/Ma%27anshan" title="Ma'anshan">Ma'anshan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> in Moukung, Western Szechuan, they encountered the 50,000-strong CCP Fourth Front Army of <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Guotao" title="Zhang Guotao">Zhang Guotao</a>, and together proceeded to Maoerhkai and then <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a>. Zhang and Mao disagreed over what to do; the latter wished to proceed to Shaanxi, while Zhang wanted to retreat east to <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a>, far from the KMT threat. It was agreed that they would go their separate ways, with <a href="/wiki/Zhu_De" title="Zhu De">Zhu De</a> joining Zhang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup> Mao's forces proceeded north, through hundreds of kilometres of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongolian-Manchurian_grassland" title="Mongolian-Manchurian grassland">Grasslands</a>, an area of quagmire where they were attacked by <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu tribesman</a> and where many soldiers succumbed to famine and disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feigon_2002_61_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feigon_2002_61-142">[141]</a></sup> Finally reaching Shaanxi, they fought off both the KMT and an Islamic cavalry militia before crossing the <a href="/wiki/Min_Mountains" title="Min Mountains">Min Mountains</a> and Mount Liupan and reaching the Shenshi Soviet; only 7,000–8000 had survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feigon_2002_61_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Feigon_2002_61-142">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> The Long March cemented Mao's status as the dominant figure in the party. In November 1935, he was named chairman of the Military Commission. From this point onward, Mao was the Communist Party's undisputed leader, even though he would not become party chairman until 1943.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Alliance_with_the_Kuomintang:_1935.E2.80.931940"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Alliance_with_the_Kuomintang:_1935–1940">Alliance with the Kuomintang: 1935–1940</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1938_Mao_Zedong_Zhang_Guotao_in_Yan%27an.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/1938_Mao_Zedong_Zhang_Guotao_in_Yan%27an.jpg/220px-1938_Mao_Zedong_Zhang_Guotao_in_Yan%27an.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/1938_Mao_Zedong_Zhang_Guotao_in_Yan%27an.jpg/330px-1938_Mao_Zedong_Zhang_Guotao_in_Yan%27an.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/1938_Mao_Zedong_Zhang_Guotao_in_Yan%27an.jpg/440px-1938_Mao_Zedong_Zhang_Guotao_in_Yan%27an.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1938_Mao_Zedong_Zhang_Guotao_in_Yan%27an.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zhang_Guotao" title="Zhang Guotao">Zhang Guotao</a> (left) and Mao Zedong in Yan'an, 1937</div></div></div>
<p>Mao's troops arrived at the <a href="/wiki/Yan%27an" title="Yan'an">Yan'an</a> Soviet during October 1935 and settled in Pao An, until spring 1936. While there, they developed links with local communities, redistributed and farmed the land, offered medical treatment, and began literacy programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feigon_2002_61_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-Feigon_2002_61-142">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schram_p193_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schram_p193-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> Mao now commanded 15,000 soldiers, boosted by the arrival of <a href="/wiki/He_Long" title="He Long">He Long</a>'s men from Hunan and the armies of Zhu De and Zhang Guotao returned from Tibet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schram_p193_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schram_p193-145">[144]</a></sup> In February 1936, they established the North West Anti-Japanese Red Army University in Yan'an, through which they trained increasing numbers of new recruits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup> In January 1937, they began the "anti-Japanese expedition", that sent groups of guerrilla fighters into Japanese-controlled territory to undertake sporadic attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schram1966_p20_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schram1966_p20-148">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup> In May 1937, a Communist Conference was held in Yan'an to discuss the situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> Western reporters also arrived in the "Border Region" (as the Soviet had been renamed); most notable were <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Snow" title="Edgar Snow">Edgar Snow</a>, who used his experiences as a basis for <i><a href="/wiki/Red_Star_Over_China" title="Red Star Over China">Red Star Over China</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Agnes_Smedley" title="Agnes Smedley">Agnes Smedley</a>, whose accounts brought international attention to Mao's cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1945_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1364" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/1945_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg/220px-1945_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/1945_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg/330px-1945_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/1945_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg/440px-1945_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1945_Mao_and_Chiang.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In an effort to defeat the Japanese, Mao (left) agreed to collaborate with Chiang (right).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mao1938a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="673" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Mao1938a.jpg/220px-Mao1938a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Mao1938a.jpg/330px-Mao1938a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Mao1938a.jpg/440px-Mao1938a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mao1938a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mao in 1938, writing <i>On Protracted War</i></div></div></div>
<p>On the Long March, Mao's wife He Zizen had been injured by a shrapnel wound to the head. She travelled to Moscow for medical treatment; Mao proceeded to divorce her and marry an actress, <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Qing" title="Jiang Qing">Jiang Qing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schram_p208_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schram_p208-152">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup> He Zizhen was reportedly "dispatched to a mental asylum in Moscow to make room" for Qing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup> Mao moved into a cave-house and spent much of his time reading, tending his garden and theorising.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup> He came to believe that the Red Army alone was unable to defeat the Japanese, and that a Communist-led "government of national defence" should be formed with the KMT and other "bourgeois nationalist" elements to achieve this goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup> Although despising Chiang Kai-shek as a "traitor to the nation",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> on 5 May, he telegrammed the Military Council of the Nanking National Government proposing a military alliance, a course of action advocated by Stalin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> Although Chiang intended to ignore Mao's message and continue the civil war, he was arrested by one of his own generals, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhang_Xueliang" title="Zhang Xueliang">Zhang Xueliang</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an_Incident" title="Xi'an Incident">Xi'an Incident</a>; Zhang forced Chiang to discuss the issue with the Communists, resulting in the formation of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_United_Front_(China)" title="Second United Front (China)">United Front</a> with concessions on both sides on 25 December 1937.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Japanese had taken both Shanghai and <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanking (Nanjing)</a>—resulting in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanking_Massacre" title="Nanking Massacre">Nanking Massacre</a>, an atrocity Mao never spoke of all his life—and was pushing the Kuomintang government inland to <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chungking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> The Japanese's brutality led to increasing numbers of Chinese joining the fight, and the Red Army grew from 50,000 to 500,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carter1976_p105_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carter1976_p105-162">[161]</a></sup> In August 1938, the Red Army formed the <a href="/wiki/New_Fourth_Army" title="New Fourth Army">New Fourth Army</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Route_Army" title="Eighth Route Army">Eighth Route Army</a>, which were nominally under the command of Chiang's <a href="/wiki/National_Revolutionary_Army" title="National Revolutionary Army">National Revolutionary Army</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup> In August 1940, the Red Army initiated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hundred_Regiments_Campaign" title="Hundred Regiments Campaign">Hundred Regiments Campaign</a>, in which 400,000 troops attacked the Japanese simultaneously in five provinces. It was a military success that resulted in the death of 20,000 Japanese, the disruption of railways and the loss of a coal mine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carter1976_p105_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carter1976_p105-162">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup> From his base in Yan'an, Mao authored several texts for his troops, including <i>Philosophy of Revolution</i>, which offered an introduction to the Marxist theory of knowledge; <i>Protracted Warfare</i>, which dealt with guerrilla and mobile military tactics; and <i>New Democracy</i>, which laid forward ideas for China's future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kang_Seng_Mao_Zedong_in_Yan%27an.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Kang_Seng_Mao_Zedong_in_Yan%27an.jpg/220px-Kang_Seng_Mao_Zedong_in_Yan%27an.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/Kang_Seng_Mao_Zedong_in_Yan%27an.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kang_Seng_Mao_Zedong_in_Yan%27an.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mao with <a href="/wiki/Kang_Sheng" title="Kang Sheng">Kang Sheng</a> in Yan'an, 1945</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Resuming_civil_war:_1940.E2.80.931949"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Resuming_civil_war:_1940–1949">Resuming civil war: 1940–1949</span></h3>
<p>In 1944, the U.S. sent a special diplomatic envoy, called the <a href="/wiki/Dixie_Mission" title="Dixie Mission">Dixie Mission</a>, to the Chinese Communist Party. The American soldiers who were sent to the mission were favourably impressed. The party seemed less corrupt, more unified, and more vigorous in its resistance to Japan than the Kuomintang. The soldiers confirmed to their superiors that the party was both strong and popular over a broad area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-166">[165]</a></sup> In the end of the mission, the contacts which the U.S. developed with the Chinese Communist Party led to very little.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-166">[165]</a></sup> After the end of World War II, the U.S. continued their diplomatic and military assistance to Chiang Kai-shek and his KMT government forces against the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="People's Liberation Army">People's Liberation Army</a> (PLA) led by Mao Zedong during the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">civil war</a> and abandoned the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Coalition_government" title="Coalition government">coalition government</a> which would include the CCP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> Likewise, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> gave support to Mao by <a href="/wiki/Soviet_occupation_of_Manchuria" title="Soviet occupation of Manchuria">occupying north-eastern China</a>, and secretly giving it to the Chinese communists in March 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PLAHuaihai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="368" data-file-width="457" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/PLAHuaihai.jpg/220px-PLAHuaihai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/PLAHuaihai.jpg/330px-PLAHuaihai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/PLAHuaihai.jpg/440px-PLAHuaihai.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PLAHuaihai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>PLA troops, supported by captured <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M5_Stuart" title="M5 Stuart">M5 Stuart</a> light tanks, attacking the Nationalist lines in 1948</div></div></div>
<p>In 1948, under direct orders from Mao, the People's Liberation Army starved out the Kuomintang forces occupying the city of <a href="/wiki/Changchun" title="Changchun">Changchun</a>. At least 160,000 civilians are believed to have perished during <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Changchun" title="Siege of Changchun">the siege</a>, which lasted from June until October. PLA lieutenant colonel Zhang Zhenglu, who documented the siege in his book <i><a href="/wiki/White_Snow,_Red_Blood" title="White Snow, Red Blood">White Snow, Red Blood</a></i>, compared it to <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a>: "The casualties were about the same. Hiroshima took nine seconds; Changchun took five months."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup> On 21 January 1949, Kuomintang forces suffered great losses in decisive battles against Mao's forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palestini2011_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palestini2011-170">[169]</a></sup> In the early morning of 10 December 1949, PLA troops laid siege to <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chongqing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">Chengdu</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, and Chiang Kai-shek fled from the mainland to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formosa" title="Formosa">Formosa</a> (Taiwan).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palestini2011_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-Palestini2011-170">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perkins2013_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perkins2013-171">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_of_China">Leadership of China</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="566" data-file-width="771" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG/220px-Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG/330px-Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG/440px-Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mao_Proclaiming_New_China.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mao Zedong declares the founding of the modern People's Republic of China on 1 October 1949</div></div></div>
<p>Mao proclaimed the establishment of The People's Republic of China from the <a href="/wiki/Tiananmen" title="Tiananmen">Gate of Heavenly Peace</a> (Tian'anmen) on 1 October 1949, and later that week declared "The Chinese people have stood up" (<span lang="zh">中国人民从此站起来了</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup> Mao went to Moscow for long talks in the winter of 1949–50. Mao initiated the talks which focused on the political and economic revolution in China, foreign policy, railways, naval bases, and Soviet economic and technical aid. The resulting treaty reflected Stalin's dominance and his willingness to help Mao.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="708" data-file-width="609" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg/220px-Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg/330px-Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg/440px-Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mao_and_Jiang_Qing_1946.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mao with his fourth wife, <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Qing" title="Jiang Qing">Jiang Qing</a>, called "Madame Mao", 1946</div></div></div>
<p>Mao pushed the Party to organise campaigns to reform society and extend control. These campaigns were given urgency in October 1950, when Mao made the decision to send the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Volunteer_Army" title="People's Volunteer Army">People's Volunteer Army</a>, a special unit of the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="People's Liberation Army">People's Liberation Army</a>, into the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> and fight as well as to reinforce the armed forces of North Korea, the <a href="/wiki/Korean_People%27s_Army" title="Korean People's Army">Korean People's Army</a>, which had been in full retreat. The United States placed a trade embargo on the People's Republic as a result of its involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, lasting until <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>'s <a href="/wiki/1972_visit_by_Richard_Nixon_to_China" title="1972 visit by Richard Nixon to China">improvements of relations</a>. At least 180 thousand Chinese troops died during the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mao directed operations to the minutest detail. As the Chairman of the Central Military Commission (CMC), he was also the Supreme Commander in Chief of the PLA and the People's Republic and Chairman of the Party. Chinese troops in Korea were under the overall command of then newly installed Premier <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Enlai" title="Zhou Enlai">Zhou Enlai</a>, with General <a href="/wiki/Peng_Dehuai" title="Peng Dehuai">Peng Dehuai</a> as field commander and political commissar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkitt_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burkitt-176">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Land_Reform" title="Chinese Land Reform">land reform campaigns</a>, large numbers of landlords and rich peasants were beaten to death at mass meetings organised by the Communist Party as land was taken from them and given to poorer peasants, which significantly reduced <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Campaign_to_Suppress_Counterrevolutionaries" title="Campaign to Suppress Counterrevolutionaries">Campaign to Suppress Counter-revolutionaries</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_Kuisong_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_Kuisong-179">[178]</a></sup> targeted bureaucratic burgeoisie, such as compradores, merchants and Kuomintang officials who were seen by the party as economic parasites or political enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup> In 1976, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">U.S. State department</a> estimated as many as a million were killed in the land reform, and 800,000 killed in the counter-revolutionary campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mao himself claimed that a total of 700,000 people were killed in attacks on "counter-revolutionaries" during the years 1950–1952.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup> Because there was a policy to select "at least one landlord, and usually several, in virtually every village for public execution",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_history_of_China_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_history_of_China-183">[182]</a></sup> the number of deaths range between 2 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_history_of_China_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_history_of_China-183">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_Kuisong_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_Kuisong-179">[178]</a></sup> and 5 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup> In addition, at least 1.5 million people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[186]</a></sup> perhaps as many as 4 to 6 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Valentino2004_121–122_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Valentino2004_121–122-188">[187]</a></sup> were sent to <a href="/wiki/Laogai" title="Laogai">"reform through labour"</a> camps where many perished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Valentino2004_121–122_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-Valentino2004_121–122-188">[187]</a></sup> Mao played a personal role in organising the mass repressions and established a system of execution quotas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup> which were often exceeded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_Kuisong_179-2"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_Kuisong-179">[178]</a></sup> He defended these killings as necessary for the securing of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mao,_Bulganin,_Stalin,_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1092" data-file-width="1663" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg/220px-Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg/330px-Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg/440px-Mao%2C_Bulganin%2C_Stalin%2C_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mao,_Bulganin,_Stalin,_Ulbricht_Tsedenbal.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mao at <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>'s 70th birthday celebration in Moscow, December 1949</div></div></div>
<p>The Mao government is credited with eradicating both consumption and production of <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a> during the 1950s using unrestrained repression and social reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCoy_opium_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCoy_opium-191">[190]</a></sup> Ten million addicts were forced into compulsory treatment, dealers were executed, and opium-producing regions were planted with new crops. Remaining opium production shifted south of the Chinese border into the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Triangle_(Southeast_Asia)" title="Golden Triangle (Southeast Asia)">Golden Triangle</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCoy_opium_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-McCoy_opium-191">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>Starting in 1951, Mao initiated two successive movements in an effort to rid urban areas of corruption by targeting wealthy capitalists and political opponents, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three-anti/five-anti_campaigns" title="Three-anti/five-anti campaigns">three-anti/five-anti campaigns</a>. Whereas the three-anti campaign was a focused purge of government, industrial and party officials, the five-anti campaign set its sights slightly broader, targeting capitalist elements in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[191]</a></sup> Workers denounced their bosses, spouses turned on their spouses, and children informed on their parents; the victims were often humiliated at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Struggle_session" title="Struggle session">struggle sessions</a>, where a targeted person would be verbally and physically abused until they confessed to crimes. Mao insisted that minor offenders be criticised and reformed or sent to labour camps, "while the worst among them should be shot". These campaigns took several hundred thousand additional lives, the vast majority via suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mao_dalai_lama-1955.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3389" data-file-width="7413" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Mao_dalai_lama-1955.jpg/240px-Mao_dalai_lama-1955.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Mao_dalai_lama-1955.jpg/360px-Mao_dalai_lama-1955.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Mao_dalai_lama-1955.jpg/480px-Mao_dalai_lama-1955.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mao_dalai_lama-1955.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mao and <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Enlai" title="Zhou Enlai">Zhou Enlai</a> meeting with <a href="/wiki/14th_Dalai_Lama" title="14th Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a> (right) and <a href="/wiki/Choekyi_Gyaltsen,_10th_Panchen_Lama" title="Choekyi Gyaltsen, 10th Panchen Lama">Panchen</a> Lama (left) to celebrate Tibetan New Year, Beijing, 1955</div></div></div>
<p>In Shanghai, suicide by jumping from tall buildings became so commonplace that residents avoided walking on the pavement near skyscrapers for fear that suicides might land on them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup> Some biographers have pointed out that driving those perceived as enemies to suicide was a common tactic during the Mao-era. In his biography of Mao, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Short" title="Philip Short">Philip Short</a> notes that Mao gave explicit instructions in the <a href="/wiki/Yan%27an_Rectification_Movement" title="Yan'an Rectification Movement">Yan'an Rectification Movement</a> that "no cadre is to be killed" but in practice allowed security chief <a href="/wiki/Kang_Sheng" title="Kang Sheng">Kang Sheng</a> to drive opponents to suicide and that "this pattern was repeated throughout his leadership of the People's Republic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deathtoll_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-deathtoll-195">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mao_Zedong_sitting.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1154" data-file-width="975" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Mao_Zedong_sitting.jpg/170px-Mao_Zedong_sitting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Mao_Zedong_sitting.jpg/255px-Mao_Zedong_sitting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Mao_Zedong_sitting.jpg/340px-Mao_Zedong_sitting.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mao_Zedong_sitting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photo of Mao Zedong sitting, published in "Quotations from Chairman Mao Tse-Tung", ca. 1955</div></div></div>
<p>Following the consolidation of power, Mao launched the First <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five-year_plans_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Five-year plans of the People's Republic of China">Five-Year Plan</a> (1953–1958), which emphasised rapid industrial development. Within industry, iron and steel, electric power, coal, heavy engineering, building materials, and basic chemicals were prioritised with the aim of constructing large and highly capital-intensive plants. Many of these plants were built with Soviet assistance and heavy industry grew rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup> Agriculture, industry and trade was organised on a collective basis (<a href="/wiki/Worker_cooperative" title="Worker cooperative">socialist cooperatives</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup> This period marked the beginning of China's rapid industrialisation and it resulted in an enormous success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>The success of the First-Five Year Plan encouraged Mao to instigate the Second Five-Year Plan in 1958. During this period, <a href="/wiki/Industrial_production" title="Industrial production">industrial output value</a> doubled; the <a href="/wiki/Gross_value_added" title="Gross value added">gross value</a> of agricultural products increased by 35 percent; <a href="/wiki/Steelmaking" title="Steelmaking">steel production</a> in 1962 was between 10.6 million tons or 12 million tons; investment in capital construction rose 5 percent; investment in capital construction was doubled and the average income of workers and farmers increased by up to 30 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>Programs pursued during this time include the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Flowers_Campaign" title="Hundred Flowers Campaign">Hundred Flowers Campaign</a>, in which Mao indicated his supposed willingness to consider different opinions about how China should be governed. Given the freedom to express themselves, liberal and intellectual Chinese began opposing the Communist Party and questioning its leadership. This was initially tolerated and encouraged. After a few months, Mao's government reversed its policy and persecuted those who had criticised the party, totalling perhaps 500,000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup> as well as those who were merely alleged to have been critical, in what is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Rightist_Movement" title="Anti-Rightist Movement">Anti-Rightist Movement</a>. Authors such as <a href="/wiki/Jung_Chang" title="Jung Chang">Jung Chang</a> have alleged that the Hundred Flowers Campaign was merely a ruse to root out "dangerous" thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p>Li Zhisui, Mao's physician, suggested that Mao had initially seen the policy as a way of weakening opposition to him within the party and that he was surprised by the extent of criticism and the fact that it came to be directed at his own leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Leap_Forward">Great Leap Forward</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Leap_Forward" title="Great Leap Forward">Great Leap Forward</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nikita_Khrushchev,_Mao_Zedong,_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="970" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg/220px-Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg/330px-Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg/440px-Nikita_Khrushchev%2C_Mao_Zedong%2C_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nikita_Khrushchev,_Mao_Zedong,_Ho_Chi_Minh_and_Soong_Ching-ling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mao with <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh" title="Ho Chi Minh">Ho Chi Minh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soong_Ching-ling" title="Soong Ching-ling">Soong Ching-ling</a> during a state dinner in Beijing, 1959</div></div></div>
<p>In January 1958, Mao launched the second <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five-year_plans_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Five-year plans of the People's Republic of China">Five-Year Plan</a>, known as the Great Leap Forward, a plan intended to turn China from an agrarian nation to an industrialised one<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup> and as an alternative model for economic growth to the Soviet model focusing on heavy industry that was advocated by others in the party. Under this economic program, the relatively small agricultural collectives that had been formed to date were rapidly merged into far larger <a href="/wiki/People%27s_commune" title="People's commune">people's communes</a>, and many of the peasants were ordered to work on massive infrastructure projects and on the production of iron and steel. Some private food production was banned, and livestock and farm implements were brought under collective ownership.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Under the Great Leap Forward, Mao and other party leaders ordered the implementation of a variety of unproven and unscientific new agricultural techniques by the new communes. The combined effect of the diversion of labour to steel production and infrastructure projects, and cyclical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_disasters" title="Natural disasters">natural disasters</a> led to an approximately 15% drop in grain production in 1959 followed by a further 10% decline in 1960 and no recovery in 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spence1999_p553_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spence1999_p553-204">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>In an effort to win favour with their superiors and avoid being purged, each layer in the party exaggerated the amount of grain produced under them. Based upon the falsely reported success, party cadres were ordered to requisition a disproportionately high amount of that fictitious harvest for state use, primarily for use in the cities and urban areas but also for export. The result, compounded in some areas by drought and in others by floods, was that farmers were left with little food for themselves and many millions starved to death in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Chinese_Famine" title="Great Chinese Famine">Great Chinese Famine</a>. The people of urban areas in China were given food stamps each month, but the people of rural areas were expected to grow their own crops and give some of the crops back to the government. The death count in rural parts of China surpassed the deaths in the urban centers. Additionally, the Chinese government continued to export food that could have been allocated to the country's starving citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-205">[204]</a></sup> The famine was a direct cause of the death of some 30 million Chinese peasants between 1959 and 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup> Furthermore, many children who became malnourished during years of hardship died after the Great Leap Forward came to an end in 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spence1999_p553_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spence1999_p553-204">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>The extent of Mao's knowledge of the severity of the situation has been disputed. Mao's physician believed that he may have been unaware of the extent of the famine, partly due to a reluctance of local officials to criticise his policies, and the willingness of his staff to exaggerate or outright fake reports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[206]</a></sup> Upon learning of the extent of the starvation, Mao vowed to stop eating meat, an action followed by his staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hong Kong-based historian <a href="/wiki/Frank_Dik%C3%B6tter" title="Frank Dikötter">Frank Dikötter</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup> in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Mao%27s_Great_Famine" title="Mao's Great Famine">Mao's Great Famine</a></i>, challenged the notion that Mao did not know about the famine throughout the country until it was too late as "largely a myth—at most partially true for the autumn of 1958 only." At a secret meeting in the Jinjiang Hotel in Shanghai dated 25 March 1959, Dikötter continues, Mao specifically ordered the party to procure up to one third of all the grain, and announced that "To distribute resources evenly will only ruin the Great Leap Forward. When there is not enough to eat, people starve to death. It is better to let half of the people die so that the other half can eat their fill."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dikottersite_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dikottersite-210">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_P._Bernstein" title="Thomas P. Bernstein">Thomas P. Bernstein</a> of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> offered his view that Mao's statement in the 25 March 1959, meeting  was "an instance of Mao's use of hyperbole, another being his casual acceptance of death of half the population during a nuclear war." In other contexts, Bernstein went on, Mao did not in fact accept mass death. In October 1958, Mao expressed real concern that 40,000 people in Yunnan had starved to death and shortly after the 25 March meeting, he worried about 25.2 million people who were at risk of starvation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilfulness_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilfulness-212">[211]</a></sup> From late summer on, Mao forgot about this issue until the Xinyang Incident came to light in October 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[212]</a></sup> Anthony Garnaut says that Dikötter's juxtaposition and sampling techniques fall short of academic best practice. He also posits that Dikötter's interpretation of Mao's quotation ("It is better to let half of the people die so that the other half can eat their fill") not only ignores the substantial commentary on the conference by other scholars and several of its key participants but defies the very plain wording of the archival document in his possession on which he hangs his case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[213]</a></sup> There is a discussion about Dikötter's misuse of Mao's quotation in <a href="/wiki/H-Net" title="H-Net">H-Net</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg/220px-People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg/330px-People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a8/People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:People%27s_commone_canteen3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early in the Great Leap Forward, commune members were encouraged to eat their fill in communal canteens, but many canteens shut down as they ran out of food and fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In late autumn 1958, Mao condemned cadres for tactics such as requiring exhausting labour acknowledge that anti-rightist pressures were a major cause of "production at the expense of livelihood." He refused to abandon the GLF to solve these difficulties, but he did demand that they be confronted. After the July 1959 <a href="/wiki/Lushan_Conference" title="Lushan Conference">clash at Lushan</a> Conference with <a href="/wiki/Peng_Dehuai" title="Peng Dehuai">Peng Dehuai</a>, Mao launched a new anti-rightist campaign along with the radical policies that he previously abandoned. Mao expressed concern about abnormal deaths and other abuses in the spring of 1960, but did not move to stop them. Bernstein concludes that the Chairman "wilfully ignored the lessons of the first radical phase for the sake of achieving extreme ideological and developmental goals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilfulness_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-wilfulness-212">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jasper_Becker" title="Jasper Becker">Jasper Becker</a> notes that Mao was dismissive of reports he received of food shortages in the countryside and refused to change course, believing that peasants were lying and that rightists and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kulaks" title="Kulaks">kulaks</a> were hoarding grain. He refused to open state granaries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Becker81_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Becker81-217">[216]</a></sup> and instead launched a series of "anti-grain concealment" drives that resulted in numerous purges and suicides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[217]</a></sup> Other violent campaigns followed in which party leaders went from village to village in search of hidden food reserves, and not only grain, as Mao issued quotas for pigs, chickens, ducks and eggs. Many peasants accused of hiding food were tortured and beaten to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mao stepped down as President of China on 27 April 1959; however, he retained other top positions such as Chairman of the Communist Party and of the Central Military Commission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-220">[219]</a></sup> The Presidency was transferred to Liu Shaoqi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-220">[219]</a></sup> He was eventually forced to abandon the policy in 1962, and he lost political power to <a href="/wiki/Liu_Shaoqi" title="Liu Shaoqi">Liu Shaoqi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[220]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Great Leap Forward was a tragedy for the vast majority of the Chinese. Although the steel quotas were officially reached, almost all of the supposed steel made in the countryside was iron, as it had been made from assorted scrap metal in home-made furnaces with no reliable source of fuel such as coal. This meant that proper <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> conditions could not be achieved. According to Zhang Rongmei, a geometry teacher in rural Shanghai during the Great Leap Forward: "We took all the furniture, pots, and pans we had in our house, and all our neighbours did likewise. We put everything in a big fire and melted down all the metal".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  The worst of the famine was steered towards enemies of the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[221]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jasper_Becker" title="Jasper Becker">Jasper Becker</a> explains: "The most vulnerable section of China's population, around five percent, were those whom Mao called '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enemies_of_the_people" title="Enemies of the people">enemies of the people</a>'. Anyone who had in previous campaigns of repression been labeled a 'black element' was given the lowest priority in the allocation of food. Landlords, rich peasants, former members of the nationalist regime, religious leaders, rightists, counter-revolutionaries and the families of such individuals died in the greatest numbers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[222]</a></sup>
</p><p>At a large Communist Party conference in Beijing in January 1962, dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/Seven_Thousand_Cadres_Conference" title="Seven Thousand Cadres Conference">Seven Thousand Cadres Conference</a>", State Chairman Liu Shaoqi denounced the Great Leap Forward, attributing the project to widespread famine in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-224">[223]</a></sup> The overwhelming majority of delegates expressed agreement, but Defense Minister <a href="/wiki/Lin_Biao" title="Lin Biao">Lin Biao</a> staunchly defended Mao.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-224">[223]</a></sup> A brief period of liberalisation followed while Mao and Lin plotted a comeback.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang_224-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chang-224">[223]</a></sup> Liu Shaoqi and <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a> rescued the economy by disbanding the people's communes, introducing elements of private control of peasant smallholdings and importing grain from Canada and Australia to mitigate the worst effects of famine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[224]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences">Consequences</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kissinger_Mao.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="349" data-file-width="499" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Kissinger_Mao.jpg/220px-Kissinger_Mao.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Kissinger_Mao.jpg/330px-Kissinger_Mao.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Kissinger_Mao.jpg/440px-Kissinger_Mao.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kissinger_Mao.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mao with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Enlai" title="Zhou Enlai">Zhou Enlai</a>, Beijing, 1972</div></div></div>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Lushan_Conference" title="Lushan Conference">Lushan Conference</a> in July/August 1959, several ministers expressed concern that the Great Leap Forward had not proved as successful as planned. The most direct of these was Minister of Defence and Korean War veteran General <a href="/wiki/Peng_Dehuai" title="Peng Dehuai">Peng Dehuai</a>. Following Peng's criticism of the Great Leap Forward, Mao orchestrated a purge of Peng and his supporters, stifling criticism of the Great Leap policies. Senior officials who reported the truth of the famine to Mao were branded as "right opportunists."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[225]</a></sup> A campaign against right-wing opportunism was launched and resulted in party members and ordinary peasants being sent to prison labour camps where many would subsequently die in the famine. Years later the CCP would conclude that as many as six million people were wrongly punished in the campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>The number of deaths by starvation during the Great Leap Forward is deeply controversial. Until the mid-1980s, when official census figures were finally published by the Chinese Government, little was known about the scale of the disaster in the Chinese countryside, as the handful of Western observers allowed access during this time had been restricted to model villages where they were deceived into believing that the Great Leap Forward had been a great success. There was also an assumption that the flow of individual reports of starvation that had been reaching the West, primarily through Hong Kong and Taiwan, must have been localised or exaggerated as China was continuing to claim record harvests and was a net exporter of grain through the period. Because Mao wanted to pay back early to the Soviets debts totalling 1.973 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_yuan" title="Chinese yuan">yuan</a> from 1960 to 1962,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_Jisheng_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_Jisheng-228">[227]</a></sup> exports increased by 50%, and fellow Communist regimes in <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">North Vietnam</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist_People%27s_Republic_of_Albania" title="Socialist People's Republic of Albania">Albania</a> were provided grain free of charge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Becker81_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-Becker81-217">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>Censuses were carried out in China in 1953, 1964 and 1982. The first attempt to analyse this data to estimate the number of famine deaths was carried out by American demographer Dr. Judith Banister and published in 1984. Given the lengthy gaps between the censuses and doubts over the reliability of the data, an accurate figure is difficult to ascertain. Nevertheless, Banister concluded that the official data implied that around 15 million excess deaths incurred in China during 1958–61, and that based on her modelling of Chinese demographics during the period and taking account of assumed under-reporting during the famine years, the figure was around 30 million. The official statistic is 20 million deaths, as given by <a href="/wiki/Hu_Yaobang" title="Hu Yaobang">Hu Yaobang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[228]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yang_Jisheng_(historian)" title="Yang Jisheng (historian)">Yang Jisheng</a>, a former <a href="/wiki/Xinhua_News_Agency" title="Xinhua News Agency">Xinhua News Agency</a> reporter who had privileged access and connections available to no other scholars, estimates a death toll of 36 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yang_Jisheng_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yang_Jisheng-228">[227]</a></sup> Frank Dikötter estimates that there were at least 45 million premature deaths attributable to the Great Leap Forward from 1958 to 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[229]</a></sup> Various other sources have put the figure at between 20 and 46 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maostats_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-maostats-233">[232]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Split_from_Soviet_Union">Split from Soviet Union</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_split" title="Sino-Soviet split">Sino-Soviet split</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:President_Gerald_Ford_and_Daughter_Susan_Watch_as_Secretary_of_State_Henry_Kissinger_Shakes_Hands_with_Mao_Tse-Tung.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4349" data-file-width="6106" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/President_Gerald_Ford_and_Daughter_Susan_Watch_as_Secretary_of_State_Henry_Kissinger_Shakes_Hands_with_Mao_Tse-Tung.jpg/220px-President_Gerald_Ford_and_Daughter_Susan_Watch_as_Secretary_of_State_Henry_Kissinger_Shakes_Hands_with_Mao_Tse-Tung.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/President_Gerald_Ford_and_Daughter_Susan_Watch_as_Secretary_of_State_Henry_Kissinger_Shakes_Hands_with_Mao_Tse-Tung.jpg/330px-President_Gerald_Ford_and_Daughter_Susan_Watch_as_Secretary_of_State_Henry_Kissinger_Shakes_Hands_with_Mao_Tse-Tung.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/President_Gerald_Ford_and_Daughter_Susan_Watch_as_Secretary_of_State_Henry_Kissinger_Shakes_Hands_with_Mao_Tse-Tung.jpg/440px-President_Gerald_Ford_and_Daughter_Susan_Watch_as_Secretary_of_State_Henry_Kissinger_Shakes_Hands_with_Mao_Tse-Tung.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:President_Gerald_Ford_and_Daughter_Susan_Watch_as_Secretary_of_State_Henry_Kissinger_Shakes_Hands_with_Mao_Tse-Tung.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> watches as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> shakes hands with Mao during their visit to China, 2 December 1975</div></div></div>
<p>On the international front, the period was dominated by the further isolation of China. The <a href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_split" title="Sino-Soviet split">Sino-Soviet split</a> resulted in <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>'s withdrawal of all Soviet technical experts and aid from the country. The split concerned the leadership of <a href="/wiki/World_communism" title="World communism">world communism</a>. The USSR had a network of Communist parties it supported; China now created its own rival network to battle it out for local control of the left in numerous countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[233]</a></sup> Lorenz M. Lüthi writes: "The Sino-Soviet split was one of the key events of the Cold War, equal in importance to the construction of the Berlin Wall, the Cuban Missile Crisis, the Second Vietnam War, and Sino-American rapprochement. The split helped to determine the framework of the Second Cold War in general, and influenced the course of the Second Vietnam War in particular."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>The split resulted from <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>'s more moderate Soviet leadership after the death of Stalin in March 1953. Only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Albania" title="People's Republic of Albania">Albania</a> openly sided with China, thereby forming an alliance between the two countries which would last until after Mao's death in 1976. Warned that the Soviets had nuclear weapons, Mao minimised the threat. Becker says that "Mao believed that the bomb was a 'paper tiger', declaring to Khrushchev that it would not matter if China lost 300 million people in a nuclear war: the other half of the population would survive to ensure victory".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[235]</a></sup> Struggle against Soviet revisionism and U.S. imperialism was an important aspect of Mao's attempt to direct the revolution in the right direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[236]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Proletarian_Cultural_Revolution">Great Proletarian Cultural Revolution</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1966-11_1966%E5%B9%B4%E6%AF%9B%E6%B3%BD%E4%B8%9C%E6%9E%97%E5%BD%AA%E4%B8%8E%E7%BA%A2%E5%8D%AB%E5%85%B5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1127" data-file-width="613" decoding="async" height="368" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/1966-11_1966%E5%B9%B4%E6%AF%9B%E6%B3%BD%E4%B8%9C%E6%9E%97%E5%BD%AA%E4%B8%8E%E7%BA%A2%E5%8D%AB%E5%85%B5.jpg/200px-1966-11_1966%E5%B9%B4%E6%AF%9B%E6%B3%BD%E4%B8%9C%E6%9E%97%E5%BD%AA%E4%B8%8E%E7%BA%A2%E5%8D%AB%E5%85%B5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/1966-11_1966%E5%B9%B4%E6%AF%9B%E6%B3%BD%E4%B8%9C%E6%9E%97%E5%BD%AA%E4%B8%8E%E7%BA%A2%E5%8D%AB%E5%85%B5.jpg/300px-1966-11_1966%E5%B9%B4%E6%AF%9B%E6%B3%BD%E4%B8%9C%E6%9E%97%E5%BD%AA%E4%B8%8E%E7%BA%A2%E5%8D%AB%E5%85%B5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/1966-11_1966%E5%B9%B4%E6%AF%9B%E6%B3%BD%E4%B8%9C%E6%9E%97%E5%BD%AA%E4%B8%8E%E7%BA%A2%E5%8D%AB%E5%85%B5.jpg/400px-1966-11_1966%E5%B9%B4%E6%AF%9B%E6%B3%BD%E4%B8%9C%E6%9E%97%E5%BD%AA%E4%B8%8E%E7%BA%A2%E5%8D%AB%E5%85%B5.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1966-11_1966%E5%B9%B4%E6%AF%9B%E6%B3%BD%E4%B8%9C%E6%9E%97%E5%BD%AA%E4%B8%8E%E7%BA%A2%E5%8D%AB%E5%85%B5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A public appearance of Chairman Mao and <a href="/wiki/Lin_Biao" title="Lin Biao">Lin Biao</a> among <a href="/wiki/Red_Guards" title="Red Guards">Red Guards</a>, in Beijing, during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a> (November 1966)</div></div></div>
<p>During the early 1960s, Mao became concerned with the nature of post-1959 China. He saw that the revolution and Great Leap Forward had replaced the old ruling elite with a new one. He was concerned that those in power were becoming estranged from the people they were to serve. Mao believed that a revolution of culture would unseat and unsettle the "ruling class" and keep China in a state of "perpetual revolution" that, theoretically, would serve the interests of the majority, rather than a tiny and privileged elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[237]</a></sup> State Chairman <a href="/wiki/Liu_Shaoqi" title="Liu Shaoqi">Liu Shaoqi</a> and General Secretary <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a> favoured the idea that Mao be removed from actual power as China's head of state and government but maintain his ceremonial and symbolic role as Chairman of the Chinese Communist Party, with the party upholding all of his positive contributions to the revolution. They attempted to marginalise Mao by taking control of economic policy and asserting themselves politically as well. Many claim that Mao responded to Liu and Deng's movements by launching the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Great Proletarian Cultural Revolution</a> in 1966. Some scholars, such as Mobo Gao, claim the case for this is overstated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[238]</a></sup> Others, such as <a href="/wiki/Frank_Dik%C3%B6tter" title="Frank Dikötter">Frank Dikötter</a>, hold that Mao launched the Cultural Revolution to wreak revenge on those who had dared to challenge him over the Great Leap Forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[239]</a></sup>
</p><p>Believing that certain liberal bourgeois elements of society continued to threaten the socialist framework, groups of young people known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Guards_(China)" title="Red Guards (China)">Red Guards</a> struggled against authorities at all levels of society and even set up their own tribunals. Chaos reigned in much of the nation, and millions were persecuted. During the Cultural Revolution, nearly all of the schools and universities in China were closed, and the young intellectuals living in cities were ordered to the countryside to be "re-educated" by the peasants, where they performed hard manual labour and other work.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Cultural Revolution led to the destruction of much of China's traditional cultural heritage and the imprisonment of a huge number of Chinese citizens, as well as the creation of general economic and social chaos in the country. Millions of lives were ruined during this period, as the Cultural Revolution pierced into every part of Chinese life, depicted by such Chinese films as <i><a href="/wiki/To_Live_(1994_film)" title="To Live (1994 film)">To Live</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Blue_Kite" title="The Blue Kite">The Blue Kite</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Farewell_My_Concubine_(film)" title="Farewell My Concubine (film)">Farewell My Concubine</a></i>. It is estimated that hundreds of thousands of people, perhaps millions, perished in the violence of the Cultural Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maostats_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-maostats-233">[232]</a></sup> This included prominent figures such as Liu Shaoqi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[242]</a></sup>
</p><p>When Mao was informed of such losses, particularly that people had been driven to suicide, he is alleged to have commented: "People who try to commit suicide—don't attempt to save them! ... China is such a populous nation, it is not as if we cannot do without a few people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[243]</a></sup> The authorities allowed the Red Guards to abuse and kill opponents of the regime. Said <a href="/wiki/Xie_Fuzhi" title="Xie Fuzhi">Xie Fuzhi</a>, national police chief: "Don't say it is wrong of them to beat up bad persons: if in anger they beat someone to death, then so be it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[244]</a></sup> In August and September 1966, there were a reported 1,772 people murdered by the Red Guards in Beijing alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was during this period that Mao chose <a href="/wiki/Lin_Biao" title="Lin Biao">Lin Biao</a>, who seemed to echo all of Mao's ideas, to become his successor. Lin was later officially named as Mao's successor. By 1971, a divide between the two men had become apparent. Official history in China states that Lin was planning a military coup or an assassination attempt on Mao. Lin Biao died on 13 September 1971, in a plane crash over the air space of Mongolia, presumably as he fled China, probably anticipating his arrest. The CCP declared that Lin was planning to depose Mao and posthumously expelled Lin from the party. At this time, Mao lost trust in many of the top CCP figures. The highest-ranking Soviet Bloc intelligence defector, Lt. Gen. <a href="/wiki/Ion_Mihai_Pacepa" title="Ion Mihai Pacepa">Ion Mihai Pacepa</a> claimed he had a conversation with <a href="/wiki/Nicolae_Ceau%C8%99escu" title="Nicolae Ceaușescu">Nicolae Ceaușescu</a>, who told him about a plot to kill Mao Zedong with the help of Lin Biao organised by the <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pacepa0_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pacepa0-247">[246]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite being considered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feminist" title="Feminist">feminist</a> figure by some and a supporter of women's rights, documents released by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Department_of_State" title="US Department of State">US Department of State</a> in 2008 show that Mao declared women to be a "nonsense" in 1973, in conversation with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a>, joking that "China is a very poor country. We don't have much. What we have in excess is women. ... Let them go to your place. They will create disasters. That way you can lessen our burdens."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[247]</a></sup> When Mao offered 10 million women, Kissinger replied by saying that Mao was "improving his offer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[248]</a></sup> Mao and Kissinger then agreed that their comments on women be removed from public records, prompted by a Chinese official who feared that Mao's comments might incur public anger if released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[249]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1969, Mao declared the Cultural Revolution to be over, although various historians in and outside of China mark the end of the Cultural Revolution—as a whole or in part—in 1976, following Mao's death and the arrest of the <a href="/wiki/Gang_of_Four" title="Gang of Four">Gang of Four</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[250]</a></sup> The Central Committee in 1981 officially declared the Cultural Revolution a "severe setback" for the PRC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[251]</a></sup> It is often looked at in all scholarly circles as a greatly disruptive period for China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chirot_1996_198_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chirot_1996_198-253">[252]</a></sup> Despite the pro-poor rhetoric of Mao's regime, his economic policies led to substantial poverty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[253]</a></sup> Some scholars, such as Lee Feigon and Mobo Gao, claim there were many great advances, and in some sectors the Chinese economy continued to outperform the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-255">[254]</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimates of the death toll during the Cultural Revolution, including civilians and Red Guards, vary greatly. An estimate of around 400,000 deaths is a widely accepted minimum figure, according to <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Meisner" title="Maurice Meisner">Maurice Meisner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[255]</a></sup> MacFarquhar and Schoenhals assert that in rural China alone some 36 million people were persecuted, of whom between 750,000 and 1.5 million were killed, with roughly the same number permanently injured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[256]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Mao:_The_Unknown_Story" title="Mao: The Unknown Story">Mao: The Unknown Story</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Jung_Chang" title="Jung Chang">Jung Chang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jon_Halliday" title="Jon Halliday">Jon Halliday</a> claim that as many as 3 million people died in the violence of the Cultural Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian Daniel Leese writes that in the 1950s Mao's personality was hardening: "The impression of Mao's personality that emerges from the literature is disturbing. It reveals a certain temporal development from a down-to-earth leader, who was amicable when uncontested and occasionally reflected on the limits of his power, to an increasingly ruthless and self-indulgent dictator. Mao's preparedness to accept criticism decreased continuously."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[258]</a></sup>
</p>
